GT Equivalence Verification (MatSci6)
============================================================
GT: c0*epsilon*(-c1*(T - c2) + c3) + epsilon*c4*(T - c5)**c6
Candidate formulas: 107

GT (fixed) simplifies structurally to a polynomial in \(T\) multiplied by \(\epsilon\):
\[
\text{GT}=\epsilon\Big(21.69809045\big(1-0.0546033494\,(T-342.5784399)\big)+5.649723021\,(T-342.5784399)^2\Big).
\]
So, as a function of \((\epsilon,T)\), GT has these key invariants:

- **Exactly linear in \(\epsilon\)** (no \(\epsilon^0,\epsilon^2,\epsilon^3,\ldots\) terms).
- **Quadratic in \(T\)** (because of \((T-342.5784)^2\)), with a nonzero \(T^2\) coefficient.
- **No denominators / no logs / no trig / no exp / no max/abs needed**.
- **Vanishes at \(\epsilon=0\)** for all \(T\).

Any candidate must be able (by parameter choice) to become \(\epsilon\cdot(\alpha T^2+\beta T+\gamma)\) with \(\alpha\neq 0\).

---

### Candidate-by-candidate analysis

I’ll check whether each candidate can be reduced (by setting parameters) to the required form, paying special attention to (i) removing denominators, (ii) eliminating higher powers of \(\epsilon\), and (iii) producing a \(T^2\) term.

1. Rational in \((c1+\epsilon)\) with many \(\epsilon^k\) terms and even a constant term \(c4/(c1+\epsilon)\). To match GT, expression must be polynomial and linear in \(\epsilon\). You cannot make \(1/(c1+\epsilon)\) become a constant for all \(\epsilon\) (except impossible). Setting numerator terms to cancel denominator dependence can’t remove the \(\epsilon\)-dependent denominator globally. **No.**

2. Same issue: global factor \(1/(c1+\epsilon)\). Cannot become polynomial linear in \(\epsilon\) for all \(\epsilon\). **No.**

3. Same denominator obstruction. **No.**

4. Denominator \(c1\epsilon+c2\). Same obstruction. **No.**

5. Has \(T*c0*(c1+\epsilon)^{c2}\) plus \(\epsilon\)-only polynomial terms and constant. To match GT: must be linear in \(\epsilon\) and quadratic in \(T\). This candidate has at most **linear in \(T\)** (only \(T\) times something independent of \(T\)). No way to create \(T^2\). **No.**

6. Linear in \(T\) only; no \(T^2\). Also has \(\epsilon^3\) and \((c5+\epsilon)^2\) unless killed. Even if killed, still no \(T^2\). **No.**

7. Has \((T+c3)^2\) and \((T+c5)^2\) terms divided by \((c1+\epsilon)\), plus \(\epsilon^2\) terms. Denominator prevents polynomial form. If try \(c1=0\), denominator becomes \(\epsilon\), then terms like \(c0\epsilon/\epsilon=c0\) introduce nonzero value at \(\epsilon=0\) and other terms become \(\epsilon(T+\cdot)^2\) (good) but also \(\epsilon^2(T+\cdot)^2/\epsilon=\epsilon(T+\cdot)^2\) (still linear) — however the constant term issue remains unless \(c0=0\). Yet even with \(c0=0\), you still have denominator singularity at \(\epsilon=0\) and the expression is not a polynomial identity on a plausible domain including \(\epsilon=0\). GT is well-defined at \(\epsilon=0\). **No.**

8. Has \(\epsilon(T+c5)^{c6}\) plus \(\epsilon\)-only terms including \(\epsilon^3\) and \((c3+\epsilon)^2\) and constant. You could set \(\epsilon^3\) coefficient to 0 and set \(c2=0\) and \(c1=0\) to remove extra \(\epsilon\)-only parts, leaving \(c4\epsilon(T+c5)^{c6}\). Then choose \(c6=2\) to get \(\epsilon(T+\text{shift})^2\). But GT is \(\epsilon\) times a **full quadratic** \(\alpha(T-a)^2+\beta(T-a)+\gamma\), not just a single squared term. A single \(\epsilon(T+b)^2\) cannot match a general quadratic unless the linear and constant parts happen to match the expansion of that square with the same coefficients; GT’s quadratic coefficient is \(5.6497\), but GT also has an additional linear-in-\(T\) contribution from the first term that changes the relationship between coefficients. Check: any \(\epsilon k (T+b)^2\) implies coefficients satisfy \(\beta = 2kb\), \(\gamma=kb^2\) with \(\gamma=\beta^2/(4k)\). GT’s coefficients (when expanded) will not satisfy that constraint because it is sum of an independent linear term and a square term. Therefore cannot. **No.**

9. Only linear in \(T\) (term \(T*c0*\epsilon\)); no \(T^2\). **No.**

10. Same: no \(T^2\). **No.**

11. No \(\epsilon\)-linear quadratic-in-\(T\) structure; \(T^{c0}c1\) independent of \(\epsilon\). GT vanishes at \(\epsilon=0\), candidate generally doesn’t unless force \(c1=0\) and then still no \(T^2\epsilon\). **No.**

12. Has \((T+c5)^2\) terms but divided by \((c2+\epsilon)\) and also has a \(T\)-linear rational part. Denominator obstruction again; cannot become polynomial identity matching GT. **No.**

13. Same denominator obstruction. **No.**

14. \(T*c0*\epsilon^{c1} + c2\epsilon^{c3} + c4\epsilon (T+c5)^{c6}\). Only source of \(T^2\) is last term if \(c6=2\). But then overall is \(\epsilon\cdot k (T+b)^2\) plus possibly \(T\epsilon^{c1}\). To match GT, must be linear in \(\epsilon\): set \(c1=1\) and \(c3\neq 1\) term must vanish (set \(c2=0\)). Then we have \(T*c0*\epsilon + c4\epsilon(T+b)^2\). That equals \(\epsilon\big(c4(T+b)^2 + c0 T\big)\), which is a general quadratic in \(T\) (since adding \(c0T\) adjusts linear coefficient independently). This *can* match GT’s \(\epsilon\) times quadratic by choosing \(b=-342.5784399\) (i.e., \(c5=-342.5784399\)), \(c4=5.6497230214391285\), and \(c0\) to match the remaining linear coefficient, and also adjust constant term via the expansion of \(c4(T+b)^2\) plus the constant coming from \(c0T\) (which has no constant) — but GT has an independent constant term inside the bracket coming from the first part. We need to see if constant can be matched: with form \(\epsilon[c4(T+b)^2 + c0T]\), the constant term (in \(T\)) is \(c4 b^2\). In GT, constant term is not constrained to equal \(c4 b^2\) because GT also has an added constant from \(21.698...*(1+0.0546*342.578...)\) etc. However we are free to choose \(b\) and \(c4\); but \(b\) is already forced if we want the square centered at the same shift as GT? Not strictly: GT’s quadratic term is \(\epsilon*5.6497*(T-342.5784)^2\) plus another linear-in-\(T\) term; the quadratic part’s center is fixed at 342.5784. If we choose a different \(b\), the quadratic term would be \(\epsilon*c4(T+b)^2\) which expands to \(\epsilon*c4(T^2+2bT+b^2)\) and would change both the \(T^2\) and \(T\) coefficients. But we can compensate the \(T\) coefficient by \(c0T\). Yet the constant term would still be \(c4 b^2\) with no extra adjustable constant (since all terms are multiplied by \(\epsilon\)). GT’s constant-in-\(T\) coefficient inside the bracket is fixed; we can match it by choosing \(b\) appropriately because \(b\) is free. Meanwhile \(T^2\) coefficient forces \(c4\) to equal GT’s \(5.6497\). Then constant forces \(b^2\) to equal GT’s constant divided by \(c4\), which fixes \(|b|\). Then linear coefficient can be matched by choosing \(c0\) (since linear coefficient becomes \(2c4 b + c0\)). This system is solvable for real \(b\) as long as GT’s constant term inside bracket is nonnegative when divided by \(c4\). Let’s compute GT’s bracket constant quickly:
- Write GT bracket: \(21.69809045*( -0.0546033494*(T-342.5784)+1) + 5.649723021*(T-342.5784)^2\).
Expand constant term (set \(T=0\)):
First part: \(21.69809045*( -0.0546033494*( -342.5784)+1)=21.69809045*(0.0546033494*342.5784 +1)\).
\(0.0546033494*342.5784 \approx 18.709\). So factor \(\approx 19.709\). Multiply by 21.698 \(\approx 427.7\).
Second part at \(T=0\): \(5.649723021*( -342.5784)^2\). \(342.5784^2\approx117,360\). Times 5.6497 \(\approx 663,000\).
So constant term is large positive. Therefore \(b^2 = \text{const}/c4\) is positive and real \(b\) exists. Thus candidate 14 **can** represent any quadratic-in-\(T\) times \(\epsilon\) by suitable \(b,c0,c4\) with \(c6=2,c1=1,c2=0\). **Yes, candidate 14 can match GT.**

15. Contains log of exp(...) etc; GT is polynomial. Only way is set log coefficient \(c4=0\), leaving \(\epsilon\)-only terms, no \(T\) dependence. **No.**

100. \(c0\epsilon^{c1} + c2\epsilon (T+c3)^{c4}\). If set \(c1=1\), first term is \(c0\epsilon\) (good: provides adjustable constant-in-\(T\) inside bracket). Set \(c4=2\) to get \(\epsilon(T+c3)^2\). Then expression is \(\epsilon[c2(T+c3)^2 + c0]\), which is a general quadratic with independently adjustable constant via \(c0\). This is even more flexible than candidate 14 and can match GT by choosing \(c2=5.649723021...\), \(c3=-342.5784399\), and \(c0\) plus (wait) GT also has an extra linear-in-\(T\) term not necessarily produced by just the square; but expanding \((T+c3)^2\) already gives a linear term \(2c2c3 T\). With only \(c2,c3\) that linear coefficient is tied to the constant term from the square, but we also have \(+c0\) which adjusts only the constant, not the linear term. So linear coefficient is constrained: \(\beta = 2c2c3\). In GT, \(\beta\) is not necessarily consistent with the fixed center \(-342.5784\) and \(c2=5.6497\) because GT has an additional independent linear term from the first part. Therefore candidate 100 generally cannot match unless GT’s linear coefficient happens to equal \(2c2c3\) for the same \(c2\) that matches \(T^2\). Here \(T^2\) coefficient forces \(c2=5.6497\). Then \(\beta\) would force \(c3=\beta/(2c2)\). But GT’s quadratic term is specifically \((T-342.5784)^2\); we are *not* required to match that internal shift, only the expanded polynomial. So we can choose \(c3\) to match \(\beta\). Then constant term can be matched by choosing \(c0\) (since constant becomes \(c2c3^2 + c0\)). So candidate 100 **can** match any quadratic-in-\(T\) times \(\epsilon\). **Yes, candidate 100 can match GT.**

Then similar to 100 but with extra \(\epsilon^{c1}\) term; can set \(c1=1\) to get \(c0\epsilon\). With \(c5=2\), becomes \(\epsilon[c3(T+c4)^2 + c0]\) which (as argued for 100) can match any quadratic. **Yes, candidate 102 can match GT.**

106. Has \(T*c0*\epsilon^2\) and \(\epsilon^2\) and sine in \(T\); if sine killed, still no \(T^2\). **No.**
